      M OMENTUM AND E RROR F EEDBACK FOR C LIPPING

009                                              A BSTRACT
010
011            Strong Differential Privacy (DP) and Optimization guarantees are two desirable
012            properties for a method in Federated Learning (FL). However, existing algorithms
013            do not achieve both properties at once: they either have optimal DP guarantees
014            but rely on restrictive assumptions such as bounded gradients/bounded data het-
015            erogeneity, or they ensure strong optimization performance but lack DP guaran-
016            tees. To address this gap in the literature, we propose and analyze a new method
017
               called Clip21-SGDM based on a novel combination of clipping, heavy-ball mo-
               mentum, and Error Feedback. In particular, for non-convex smooth distributed
018
               problems with clients having arbitrarily heterogeneous data, we prove that Clip21-
019
               SGDM has optimal convergence rate and also optimal (local-)DP neighborhood.
020            Our numerical experiments on non-convex logistic regression and training of neu-
021            ral networks highlight the superiority of Clip21-SGDM over baselines in terms of
022            the optimization performance for a given DP-budget.
023
024
025   1   I NTRODUCTION
026
027   Federated Learning (Konečný et al., 2016; McMahan et al., 2017a) is a modern training paradigm
028   where multiple (possibly heterogeneous) clients aim to jointly train a machine learning model with-
029
      out sacrificing the privacy of their own data. This setup presents several noticeable challenges
      in terms of algorithm design affecting different aspects of training, including communication ef-
030
      ficiency, partial participation of clients, data heterogeneity, security, and privacy (Kairouz et al.,
031
      2021; Wang et al., 2021). As a result, numerous optimization methods for Federated Learning (FL)
032   have been introduced in recent years. However, despite extensive research in the field, achieving
033   both strong optimization convergence and robust differential privacy (DP) guarantees (Dwork et al.,
034   2014) simultaneously in an FL algorithm remains challenging due to the conflicting nature of these
035   objectives. Indeed, most of the results in the field of DP are obtained by adding noise (e.g. Gaussian
036   noise) to the method’s update (Abadi et al., 2016; Chen et al., 2020) in order to protect the client’s
037   data that could be potentially reconstructed from the updates. Unfortunately, this approach results
038   in less accurate updates, which negatively affects the convergence. Moreover, to ensure DP, this
039   mechanism should be applied to the method with bounded updates, which is typically achieved via
040
      gradient clipping (Pascanu et al., 2013).
041   Further complicating the issue, naïve distributed Clipped Gradient Descent (Clip-GD) is not guaran-
042   teed to converge (Khirirat et al., 2023) when clients have heterogeneous data (even in the absence
043   of any additive DP-noise), which is a common scenario in FL. To address this issue Khirirat et al.
044   (2023) apply the EF21 mechanism – originally developed by Richtárik et al. (2021) for contractive
045   compression operators to improve the standard Error Feedback (Seide et al., 2014) – to Clip-GD,
046
      resulting in a method known as Clip21-GD. Khirirat et al. (2023) show that in contrast to Clip-GD,
      Clip21-GD converges with O(1/T ) rate for smooth non-convex problems with arbitrary heteroge-
047
      neous data on clients. However, their analysis is limited to the case of full-batched gradients and
048
      does not work with DP-noise. This leads us to the natural question:
049
050          Is it possible to design a method that combines both strong optimization performance
051                                 and DP guarantees in a stochastic setting?
052
053   Our contribution. In this paper, we provide a positive answer to the above question by introduc-
      ing a new method, named Clip21-SGDM, which incorporates clipping, error feedback and heavy-ball


                                                        1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      momentum (Polyak, 1964) in a novel way. For smooth non-convex distributed optimization prob-
055   lems, we show that Clip21-SGDM (i) converges with optimal O(1/T ) rate when the workers compute
056                                                e 1/√nT ) high-probability convergence rate when the
      full gradients, (ii) converges with optimal O(
057   workers use stochastic gradients with sub-Gaussian noise, and (iii) has optimal local DP-error when
058   DP-noise is added to the clients’ updates. We also prove that Clip21-SGD is not guaranteed to con-
059   verge in the stochastic case, underscoring the need for changes in the algorithm. Our experiments
060   on logistic regression and neural networks highlight the robustness of Clip21-SGDM to the choice of
061   clipping level and indicate Clip21-SGDM’s superiority over Clip-SGD and Clip21-SGD in terms of
062   optimization performance for a given DP-budget.
063
064   1.1      P ROBLEM F ORMULATION AND A SSUMPTIONS
065
066   We consider the optimization problem of the form
                                            "                  n
                                                                        #
067
                                                            1X
068                                            min f (x) :=       fi (x)                                            (1)
                                               x∈Rd         n i=1
069
070   that typically appears in many machine learning applications and is standard for Federated Learning.
071   Here x denotes the parameters of a model, fi represents the loss associated with the local dataset Di
072   of worker i ∈ [n], and f is an average loss across all workers participating in the training process.
073
      We make two main assumptions on the problem. The first one is smoothness, which is standard for
074   non-convex optimization (Carmon et al., 2020; Danilova et al., 2022). In addition, we also assume
075   that f (x) is uniformly lower bounded since otherwise, problem (1) is intractable.
076
      Assumption 1. We assume that each individual loss function fi is L-smooth, i.e., for any x, y ∈ Rd
077   and i ∈ [n] we have
078                                    ∥∇fi (x) − ∇fi (y)∥ ≤ L∥x − y∥.                                (2)
079
      Moreover, we assume that f ∗ := inf x∈Rd f (x) > −∞.
080
081   We also note that our analysis can be easily generalized to the case when L depends on fi .
082
      Next, since computation of the full gradients is expensive in many practical applications, it is natural
083
      to consider the case when clients compute stochastic gradients. We make the following assumption
084
      on the stochastic noise of these gradients.
085
086
      Assumption 2. We assume that each worker i has access to a σ-sub-Gaussian unbiased estimator
      ∇fi (x, ξ) of a local gradient ∇fi (x), i.e., for some1 σ ≥ 0 and any x ∈ Rd and ∀i ∈ [n] we have
087
                                                                   t 2

088                         E [∇fi (x, ξ)] = ∇fi (x), E exp ∥θi ∥ /σ2 ≤ exp(1),                            (3)
089
      where ξ denotes the source of the stochasticity and θi := ∇fi (x, ξ) − ∇fi (x).
090
091   Although this assumption is stronger than bounded variance, it is standard for the high-probability2
092   analysis of SGD-type methods with polylogarithmic dependence on the confidence level (Ne-
093   mirovski et al., 2009; Ghadimi & Lan, 2012). The second part of (3) is equivalent to Pr (∥θit ∥ ≥ b) ≤
                2

094   2 exp −b /(2σ ) up to a constant factor in σ 2 (Vershynin, 2018). We also note that it is possible
                      2

095   to show high-probability bounds for SGD-type methods with polylogarithmic dependence on the
096   confidence level when the noise has sub-Weibull tails (Madden et al., 2024), i.e., the noise can be
097   even heavier but it affects the polylogarithmic factors.
098   Finally, we provide two important definitions for this work. The first one is the definition of the
099   clipping operator, which is a non-linear map from Rd to Rd parameterized by the clipping thresh-
100   old/level τ > 0 and defined as
101                                               (

                                                       x, if ∥x∥ > τ,
102                                   clipτ (x) := ∥x∥                                                (4)
103                                                 x,       if ∥x∥ ≤ τ.
104
      Next, we will use the following classical definition of (ε, δ)-Differential Privacy, which introduces
105   plausible deniability into the output of a learning algorithm.
106
         1
107          For simplicity, we define 0/0 := 0. Then, (3) with σ = 0 implies ∇fi (x, ξ) = ∇fi (x) almost surely.
         2
             We elaborate on the reasons why we focus on high-probability analysis in Section 3.2.


                                                              2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      Definition 1 ((ε, δ)-Differential Privacy (Dwork et al., 2014)). A randomized method M : D → R
109   satisfies (ε, δ)-Differential Privacy ((ε, δ)-DP) if for any adjacent D, D′ ∈ D (e.g., if D and D′ are
110   datasets, then the adjacency means that D and D′ differ in 1 sample) and for any S ⊆ R
111
112                               Pr (M(D) ∈ S) ≤ eε Pr (M(D′ ) ∈ S) + δ.                                 (5)
113
114   In this definition, the smaller ε, δ are, the more private the method is. Intuitively, if inequality (5)
115   holds with small values of ε and δ, it becomes difficult to infer the specific data point that differs
116   between two similar datasets based solely on the output of M.
117
118   1.2   R ELATED W ORK
119
120   Differential Privacy. The most common approach to obtaining DP guarantees is to clip each
121
      client’s update, i.e., by bounding their ℓ2 norm, and adding a calibrated amount of Gaussian noise to
      each update or the average. This is typically sufficient to obscure the influence of any single client
122
      (McMahan et al., 2017b). Commonly, two scenarios of the DP model are considered: the central
123
      model and the local model. In the first setting, central privacy, a trusted server collects updates and
124   adds noise only before updating the server-side model. This ensures that client data remains private
125   from external parties. In the second setting, local privacy, client data is protected even from the
126   server by clipping and adding noise to updates locally before sending them to the server, ensuring
127   privacy from both the server and other clients (Kasiviswanathan et al., 2011; Allouah et al., 2024).
128   The local privacy setting offers stronger privacy against untrusted servers but results in poorer learn-
129   ing performance due to the need for more noise to obscure individual updates (Chan et al., 2012;
130   Duchi et al., 2018). This can be improved by using a secure shuffler (Erlingsson et al., 2019; Balle
131   et al., 2019), which permutes updates, or a secure aggregator (Bonawitz et al., 2017), which sums
132
      updates before sending them to the server. These methods anonymize updates and enhance privacy
      while maintaining reasonable learning performance, even without a fully trusted server. Finally,
133
      (Chaudhuri et al., 2022; Hegazy et al., 2024) show that when DP is required, one can also achieve
134
      compression of updates for free.
135
136   In this work, we adopt the local DP model by injecting Gaussian noise into each client’s update.
137   However, the average noise can also be viewed as noise added to the average update. Therefore,
138
      Clip21-SGDM is compatible with all the aforementioned techniques and can also be applied to the
      central DP model with a smaller amount of noise.
139
140
141   Distributed methods with clipping. In the single-node regime, Clip-SGD has been analyzed un-
142
      der various assumptions by many authors (Zhang et al., 2020b;c;a; Gorbunov et al., 2020a; Cutkosky
      & Mehta, 2021; Sadiev et al., 2023; Liu et al., 2023). Of course, these results can be generalized to
143
      the multi-node case if clipping is applied to the aggregated (e.g. averaged) vector, although mini-
144
      batching requires a refined analysis when the noise is heavy-tailed(Kornilov et al., 2024). However,
145   to get DP, clipping has to be applied to the vectors communicated by clients to the server. In this
146   regime, Clip-SGD is not guaranteed to converge even without any stochastic noise in the gradients
147   (Chen et al., 2020; Khirirat et al., 2023). There exist several approaches to bypass this limitation
148   that can be split into two lines of work. The first one relies on explicit or implicit assumptions about
149   bounded heterogeneity. More precisely, Liu et al. (2022) analyze a version of Local-SGD/FedAvg
150   (Mangasarian, 1995; McMahan et al., 2017a) with gradient clipping for homogeneous data case as-
151   suming that the stochastic gradients have symmetric distribution around their mean and Wei et al.
152   (2020) consider Local-SGD with clipping of the models and analyze its convergence under bounded
153
      heterogeneity assumption. Moreover, the boundedness of the stochastic gradient is another assump-
      tion used in the literature but it implies the boundedness of gradients’ heterogeneity of clients as
154
      well. This assumption is used in numerous works, including: i) Zhang et al. (2022) in the analysis
155
      of a version of FedAvg with clipping of model difference (also empirically studied by Geyer et al.
156   (2017)), ii) Noble et al. (2022) who propose and analyze a version of SCAFFOLD (Karimireddy
157   et al., 2020) with gradient clipping (DP-SCAFFOLD), iii) Li & Chi (2023) who propose and ana-
158   lyze a version of BEER (Li et al., 2021) with gradient clipping (PORTER) under bounded gradient
159   and/or bounded data heterogeneity assumption, and iv) Allouah et al. (2024) who study a version
160   of Gossip-SGD (Nedic & Ozdaglar, 2009) with gradient clipping (DECOR). Although most of the
161   mentioned works have rigorous DP guarantees, the corresponding methods are not guaranteed to
      converge for arbitrary heterogeneous problems.


                                                         3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      The second line of work focuses on the clipping of shifted (stochastic) gradient. In particular, Khiri-
163   rat et al. (2023) proposed and analyzed Clip21-GD, which is based on the application of EF21
164   (Richtárik et al., 2021) to the clipping operator, and Gorbunov et al. (2024) develop and analyze
165   methods that apply clipping to the difference of stochastic gradients and learnable shift – an idea
166   that was initially proposed by Mishchenko et al. (2019) to handle data heterogeneity in the Dis-
167   tributed Learning with unbiased communication compression. However, the analysis from (Khirirat
168   et al., 2023) is limited to the noiseless regime, i.e., full-batched gradients are computed on work-
169   ers, and both of the mentioned works do not provide3 DP guarantees. We also note that clipping
170   of gradient differences is helpful in tolerating Byzantine attacks in the partial participation regime
171
      (Malinovsky et al., 2023).
172
173   Error Feedback. Error Feedback (EF) (Seide et al., 2014) is a popular technique for incorporating
174   communication compression into Distributed/Federated Learning. However, for non-convex smooth
175
      problems, the existing analysis of EF is provided either for the single-node case or relies on restric-
      tive assumptions such as boundedness of the gradient/compression error or boundedness of the data
176
      heterogeneity (gradient dissimilarity) (Stich et al., 2018; Stich & Karimireddy, 2019; Karimireddy
177
      et al., 2019; Koloskova et al., 2019; Beznosikov et al., 2023; Tang et al., 2019; Xie et al., 2020;
178   Sahu et al., 2021). Moreover, the convergence bounds for EF also depend on the data heterogeneity,
179   which is not an artifact of the analysis as illustrated in the experiments on strongly convex problems
180   Gorbunov et al. (2020b). Richtárik et al. (2021) address this limitation and propose a new version
181   of Error Feedback called EF21. However, the existing analysis of EF21-SGD requires the usage of
182   large batch sizes to achieve any predefined accuracy (Fatkhullin et al., 2021). It turns out that the
183   large batch size requirement is unavoidable for EF21-SGD to converge, but this issue can be fixed
184   using momentum (Fatkhullin et al., 2024). Momentum is also helpful in the decentralized extensions
185   of Error Feedback (Yau & Wai, 2022; Huang et al., 2023; Islamov et al., 2024).
186

188

235   To address the non-convergence of Clip-GD, Khirirat et al. (2023) propose Clip21-GD that applies
236   the clipping operator to the difference between ∇fi (xt+1 ) and the shift git , which is designed to
237   approximate ∇fi (xt ). In the deterministic case, this strategy ensures that after a certain number of
238   steps, clipping turns off on all clients since ∥∇fi (xt+1 ) − git ∥ becomes smaller than τ for all i ∈
239   [n] eventually. However, when workers compute stochastic gradients instead of the full gradients,
240   Clip21-SGD can be non-convergent as well. To illustrate this, we consider the ideal version of
241   Clip21-SGD with stochastic gradients, i.e., instead of git , we use ∇fi (xt+1 ) as a shift:

265   3             Clip21-SGDM : N EW M ETHOD AND T HEORETICAL R ESULTS

267   This section introduces Clip21-SGDM (Algorithm 3), a novel distributed method with clipping that
268   can be viewed as an enhanced version of Clip21-SGD, integrating momentum and DP-noise. That is,
269
      to control the noise coming from the stochastic gradients, we introduce momentum buffers {vit }i∈[n]
      on the clients and clip {vit+1 − git }i∈[n] in contrast to the stochastic version of Clip21-SGD that

286   applies clipping to potentially noisier vectors {∇fi (xt+1 , ξit+1 ) − git }i∈[n] . Moreover, similarly to
287   Clip21-SGD – which can be seen as EF21 (Richtárik et al., 2021) where the compression operator is
288   replaced by clipping – Clip21-SGDM can also be interpreted as EF21M (Fatkhullin et al., 2024) with
289   the same replacement. However, both EF21 and EF21M rely on the contractiveness property of the
290   compression operator C(x), i.e., the (randomized) mapping C : Rd → Rd should satisfy
                           E ∥C(x) − x∥2 ≤ (1 − ν)∥x∥2 for some ν ∈ (0, 1],

      but there is a significant difference: if ∥x∥ > τ , the contraction factor is dependent of x and can
299   be arbitrarily close to 1. To circumvent this issue, Khirirat et al. (2023) prove via induction that
300   for all iterates of Clip21-SGD, the vectors ∇fi (xt+1 ) − git have norms bounded by some constant
301   depending on the starting point. We show that a similar statement holds for Clip21-SGDM when the
302   clients compute full-batched gradients and no DP-noise is added, and we start our analysis with this
303   important special case. We also present the results in the stochastic case with and without DP noise.

305   3.1   A NALYSIS IN THE D ETERMINISTIC C ASE
306
307   The next result derives a convergence rate for Clip21-SGDM when ∇fi (xt+1 , ξit+1 ) ≡ ∇fi (xt )
308   almost surely, i.e., Assumption 2 holds with σ = 0.
309   Theorem 2 (Simplified). Let Assumptions 1 and 2 with σ = 0 hold. Let B := maxi ∥∇fi (x0 )∥ >
310   3τ and ∆ ≥ f (x0 ) − f ∗ . Then there exists a stepsize γ ≤ 1/12L and momentum parameter β = 4Lγ
311   such that the iterates of Clip21-SGDM (Algorithm 3) converge with the rate
312                                        T −1
                                        1 X                         L∆
313                                             ∥∇f (xt )∥2 ≤ O           .                        (10)
314                                     T t=0                        T
315
      Moreover, after at most 2B/τ iterations, the clipping will eventually be turned off for all workers.
316
317
      Proof sketch. The proof of Theorem 2 (and all following ones) relies on the same Lyapunov function
318   that is used by Fatkhullin et al. (2024) in the analysis of EF21M:
319
                                    n                      n
320                            γ1X t                 4γβ 1 X t                 γ
       Φt := f (xt ) − f ∗ +           ∥gi − vit ∥2 + 2      ∥v − ∇fi (xt )∥2 + ∥v t − ∇f (xt )∥2 . (11)
321                            η n i=1                η n i=1 i                β
322
323   In the definition of Φt , the only parameter that was not introduced earlier in the paper is η, and
      it hides the main technical difficulty of the proof. That is, by induction we prove that ∥vit+1 −


                                                          6
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


324
      git ∥ ≤ τ/η for some η defined in the proof. This bound is essential in deriving a descent of each
325   term in the Lyapunov function. In view of (9) and (8), this allows us to consider clipping as a
326   contractive compression operator for vectors vit+1 −git generated by the method, and also this allows
327   us to use the same Lyapunov function as in the analysis of EF21M. We defer the detailed proof to
328   Appendix D.
329
330   The above result establishes a O(1/T ) convergence rate that is optimal for non-convex smooth first-
331   order optimization (Carmon et al., 2020; 2021). This result matches the one obtained by Khirirat
332   et al. (2023), and, in particular, similarly to Clip21-SGD, Clip21-SGDM turns off clipping on each
333   client after a finite number of steps t satisfying ∥vit+1 − git ∥ ≤ τ . We also emphasize that Theorem 2
334   holds without bounded heterogeneity/gradient assumption. In contrast, even with bounded hetero-
335   geneity/gradient assumption, many existing convergence results in the non-convex case (Liu et al.,
336
      2022; Zhang et al., 2022; Li & Chi, 2023; Allouah et al., 2024) do not recover the O(1/T ) rate in the
      noiseless regime.
337
338
339   3.2   A NALYSIS IN THE S TOCHASTIC C ASE WITHOUT DP-N OISE
340
      Next, we turn to the stochastic setting where each worker has access to local gradient estimators
341
      satisfying Assumption 2. For simplicity, we first consider the case when no DP noise is added.
342
      Theorem 3. Let Assumptions 1 and 2 hold and α ∈ (0, 1). Let B         e := maxi ∥∇fi (x0 )∥ > 3τ
343
344   and ∆ ≥ Φ0 . Then there exists a stepsize γ and momentum parameter β such that the iterates of
      Clip21-SGDM (Algorithm 3) satisfy with probability at least 1 − α
345
346                           T −1                               √                !
                           1 X            t 2          L∆ σ( L∆ + B        e + σ)
347                                ∥∇f (x )∥ ≤ O e         +          √             ,                (12)
348
                           T t=0                        T               Tn
349
350
      where O
            e hides constant and logarithmic factors, and higher order terms that decrease in T .
351
352   Proof sketch. The core of the proof is similar to the one of Theorem 2. However, in contrast to
353
      the deterministic case, the vectors vit+1 − git are stochastic, meaning that under Assumption 2, they
      can have arbitrarily large norms. Therefore, we focus on the high-probability analysis and prove by
354
      induction that the vectors vit+1 − git are bounded with high probability, meaning that clipping can
355
      be seen as a contractive compressor with high probability for the vectors vit+1 − git generated by the
356
      method. The proof is also based on a refined estimation of sums of martingale difference sequences;
357   see the details in Appendix G.
358
359                                                                             √
      This result demonstrates that Clip21-SGDM achieves an optimal O(1/ nT ) (Arjevani et al., 2023)
360
      rate in the stochastic setting. In contrast to the previous works establishing similar rates (Liu et al.,
361   2022; Noble et al., 2022; Allouah et al., 2024), our result does not rely on the boundedness of the
362   gradients or data heterogeneity. Moreover, when σ = 0 (no stochastic noise), the rate from (12)
363   becomes O(1/T ), recovering the one given by Theorem 2.
364

370   DP-noise variance σω2 with probability at least 1 − α satisfy

400   To obtain local (ε, δ)-DP guarantees we follow Theorem 1 in (Abadi et al., 2016). This privacy-
401   utility trade-off matches the known lower bound for locally private algorithms (Duchi et al., 2018).
402   Overall, Theorems 2 and 3 and Corollary 1 indicate that Clip21-SGDM achieves optimal convergence
403   rates in both deterministic and stochastic regime, and also has an optimal privacy-utility trade-off.
404   These results are derived without assuming the boundedness of the gradients/data heterogeneity.

      4                            E XPERIMENTS
407
408
      In this section, we provide an empirical evaluation of the proposed algorithm against baselines such
409   as Clip21-SGD (Khirirat et al., 2023) and Clip-SGD. The learning rate and momentum (for Clip21-
410   SGDM) are tuned in all experiments. We refer to Appendix H for the detailed description of tuning.
411
412
      4.1                                   S TOCHASTIC S ETTING
413
414   First, we test the convergence of Clip-SGD, Clip21-SGD, and the proposed Clip21-SGDM algorithms
415   with stochastic gradients for various clipping radii τ on several workloads. These results demon-
416   strate the significance of using the momentum technique to achieve better performance.
417
418   4.1.1                                      N ON - CONVEX L OGISTIC R EGRESSION
419
420   We demonstrate the performance of all algorithms without adding noise for privacy but with stochas-
421
      tic gradients. We consider two cases: adding Gaussian noise to full local gradient ∇fi (x) and
      mini-batch stochastic gradient. We conduct experiments on logistic regression with non-convex reg-
422
                                     1
                                       Pm                        ⊤
                                                                           Pd      x2l
423   ularization, namely, fi (x) = m    j=1 log(1 + exp(−bij aij x)) + λ     l=1 1+x2l which is a typical
424   problem considered in previous works (Khirirat et al., 2023; Li & Chi, 2023). We use the Duke and
425   Leukemia LibSVM (Chang & Lin, 2011) datasets.
426   We plot the gradient norm averaged across the last 100 iterations and 3 different runs in Figure 2.
427   The results demonstrate the resilience of Clip21-SGDM to the choice of the clipping radius τ : it
428   achieves a smaller or similar gradient norm compared to two other algorithms over all values of τ.
429   This is especially visible when the clipping radius τ is small. These experimental findings align
430   with the theoretical results presented in this work. Besides, the convergence plots are presented in
431   Figure 7. The results demonstrate faster convergence for Clip21-SGDM than that of Clip21-SGD and
      Clip-SGD.

451
452
      4.1.2                           T RAINING R ESNET 20 AND VGG16
453
454   Next, we conduct experiments in training Resnet20 (He et al., 2016) and VGG16 (Simonyan &
455   Zisserman, 2014) models on CIFAR10 dataset (Krizhevsky et al., 2009)4 . The results are averaged
456   across 3 different random seeds and shown in Figure 3 (the clipping operator is applied on all weights
457   simultaneously) and Figure 4 (the clipping operator is applied layer-wise). We plot the test accuracy
458
      and train loss at the last point of the training. The results show that the performance of Clip-SGD
      consistently deteriorates as the clipping radius τ decreases, while Clip21-SGD and Clip21-SGDM are
459
      more stable to the changes of τ. Moreover, Clip21-SGDM outperforms Clip21-SGD for small values
460
      of τ reaching smaller train loss and larger test accuracy that supports the theoretical claims of this
461   paper. For the convergence curves we refer to Figures 8 to 11.
462
463
      4.2                            A DDING G AUSSIAN N OISE FOR DP
464
465   In the second set of experiments, we test the performance of algorithms with additive Gaussian
466   noise to preserve privacy. Since DP noise variance σω typically scales with the clipping radius τ
467   (e.g., see Corollary 1), we conduct the following set of experiments: we fix a noise-clipping ratio
468   from {0.1, 1.0, 10} for logistic regression and from {0.1, 0.3, 1.0, 3.0, 10.0} for neural networks,
469   and find such τ that gives the lowest final gradient norm, train loss, or test accuracy depending on
470   the considered workload. The high values of the noise-clipping ratio correspond to stronger DP
471
      guarantees, while low values stand for weaker DP guarantees.
472
473
      4.2.1                           N ON - CONVEX L OGISTIC R EGRESSION
474   We provide the convergence results for non-convex logistic regression in Figure 5 where the gradient
475   norm is averaged over the last 100 iterations and 5 random seeds. We demonstrate that Clip21-
476   SGDM can achieve a smaller gradient norm for all values of the noise-clipping ratio than Clip-SGD.
477   Besides, the performance of Clip21-SGD does not improve even if the noise-clipping ratio is small,
478   demonstrating the importance of the use of momentum.
479
480   4.2.2                           T RAINING N EURAL N ETWORKS WITH DP N OISE
481
      Next, we conduct experiments on training CNN and MLP models on MNIST dataset (Deng, 2012)
482
      varying the noise-clipping ratio. We highlight that it is a standard experiment setting considered in
483   the literature on differential privacy (Papernot et al., 2020; Li & Chi, 2023; Allouah et al., 2024).
484   The performance results are reported in Figure 6. We observe that no algorithm outperforms others
485

512
513
514   5                             C ONCLUSION AND F UTURE W ORK
515
516   In this work, we introduced a new method called Clip21-SGDM and proved that it achieves an op-
517   timal convergence rate and optimal privacy-utility trade-off without assuming boundedness of the
518
      gradients or boundedness of the data heterogeneity. Notably, several interesting directions remain
      unexplored. The first one is related to the generalization of the derived results to the case when
519
      stochastic gradients have heavy-tailed noise. Next, it would be interesting to study AdaGrad/Adam-
520
      type (Streeter & McMahan, 2010; Duchi et al., 2011; Kingma & Ba, 2014) versions of Clip21-SGDM
521   due to their practical superiority over SGD in solving Deep Learning problems. Finally, it is impor-
522   tant to extend the current analysis of Clip21-SGDM to the case when generalized smoothness is
523   satisfied (Zhang et al., 2020b).
524

3486   H                          E XPERIMENTS D ETAILS AND M ORE
3487
3488   H.1                            E XPERIMENTS WITH L OGISTIC R EGRESSION
3489
3490   H.1.1                             S TOCHASTIC S ETTING VARYING C LIPPING R ADIUS
3491
       We conduct experiments on non-convex logistic regression with regularization parameter λ = 10−3
3492   for 104 iterations. We use Duke and Leukemia datasets from LibSVM library and split the dataset
3493   into n = 4 equal parts. We normalize the row of the feature matrix to demonstrate the differences
3494   between algorithms. To simulate the stochastic gradients we either add centered Gaussian noise
3495   with variance σ = 0.05 for the Duke dataset and σ = 0.1 for the Leukemia dataset, or mini-batch
3496   gradients with batch-size of 13 of the whole local dataset for Duke dataset and 41 of the whole lo-
3497   cal dataset for Leukemia dataset. For Clip21-SGD and Clip-SGD algorithms, we tune the stepsize
3498   in {2−5 , . . . , 25 } and choose the one that gives the lowest final gradient norm in average across 3
3499   random seeds. For Clip21-SGDM, we tune both the stepsize in {2−5 , . . . , 25 } and the momentum
3500
       parameter in {0.1, 0.5, 0.9} and choose the best pair of parameters similarly as before. For com-
       pleteness, we report the convergence curves in Figure 7. We observe that Clip21-SGDM is more
3501
       robust to the choice of the clipping radius τ while Clip-SGD converges well only for large enough
3502
       τ . Besides, Clip21-SGD does not converge in all cases which is also highlighted by our theory in
3503   Theorem 1.
3504
3505
       H.1.2                             S TOCHASTIC S ETTING WITH ADDITIVE DP N OISE
3506
3507   We describe the setting in more detail for completeness. First, note that we use the same set of
3508   problem parameters as in Appendix H.1.1 such as n, λ, σ, and batch-size. Next, we fix a ratio
3509   between Gaussian DP noise variance σω and the clipping parameter τ from {0.1, 1.0, 10.0}. For
       a given ratio, we tune Clip21-SGD and Clip-SGD algorithms across all possible pairs of the step-

3550   H.2                            E XPERIMENTS WITH N EURAL N ETWORKS
3551
3552   H.2.1                           VARYING C LIPPING R ADIUS τ
3553
3554   Now we switch to the training of Resnet20 and VGG16 models on CIFAR10 dataset. For
3555   all algorithms, we do not use any techniques such as learning rate schedule, warm-up, or
3556   weight decay. However, we do tuning of the learning rate for Clip-SGD and Clip21-SGD from
3557   {10−3 , 10−2 , 10−1 , 100 } and choose the one that gives the highest test accuracy. For Clip21-SGDM
3558   we tune both the learning rate from {10−3 , 10−2 , 10−1 , 100 } and the momentum parameter from
3559   {0.1, 0.5, 0.9} and choose the pair that reaches the highest test accuracy. The batch size for all al-
3560
       gorithms is set to 32. We compare the performance of algorithms in two cases: when the clipping is
       applied globally on the whole model and layer-wise.
3561
3562   We observe in Figures 8 to 11 that the performance of Clip-SGD gets worsen once the clipping radius
3563   is small enough. For Clip21-SGDM is more robust to the choice of τ and can achieve smaller train
       loss and test accuracy even when τ is small.

3595   H.2.2                          A DDING A DDITIVE DP N OISE
3596
       We consider the same experiment section described in Appendix H.1.2 but now in the training of
3597   MLP and CNN models on MNIST dataset.
3598
3599   We use MLP model with 1 hidden layer of size 256 and Tanh activation function. CNN model has
3600
       2 convolution layers with 16 convolutions each and kernel size 5 with one max-pooling layer and
       Tanh activation function. We perform a grid search over the learning rate from {10−3 , . . . , 100 } and
3601
       the clipping radius from {10−4 , . . . , 10−1 }. The aforementioned tuning is performed for each value
3602
       of the noise-clipping ratio from {0.1, 0.3, 1.0, 3.0, 10.0}. he momentum parameter is tuned over
3603   {0.5, 0.1, 0.01}. We highlight that we do not use the techniques such as a learning rate scheduler
3604   although it might improve the performance of algorithms. The batch size for all algorithms is set to
3605   64.
3606
       In Figures 12 to 15 we demonstrate that Clip-SGD and Clip-SGDM always outperform Clip21-SGD.
3607
       However, there is no clear separation between Clip21-SGDM and Clip-SGD: in some cases, the latter
3608
       has better performance, and in some cases — the former.
